    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

    <a title="Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7975218-monica"><img alt="Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330487529p2/7975218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/334058212?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica" href="/user/show/7975218-monica">Monica</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer334058212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11710251678952667808">I LOVED this book. It was our May Book Club selection and I was pretty lukewarm about it as a choice,but...it turned into one of those gems I never would have read on my own. Roberts lived in Kennebunkport ,ME. and a book club member has a house there and that was our venue. We wanted to read a book with Maine as a theme and this was GREAT. The genre is historical fiction.Basically it tells the story of a young man in love going to get back his girl who has been kidnapped. But,it is sooo much mo</span>
  <span id="freeText11710251678952667808" style="display:none">I LOVED this book. It was our May Book Club selection and I was pretty lukewarm about it as a choice,but...it turned into one of those gems I never would have read on my own. Roberts lived in Kennebunkport ,ME. and a book club member has a house there and that was our venue. We wanted to read a book with Maine as a theme and this was GREAT. The genre is historical fiction.Basically it tells the story of a young man in love going to get back his girl who has been kidnapped. But,it is sooo much more. Details about the settler's life,their relationships with the Indians, (P.S.-the Abenakis saved the settler's butts on more than one occasion) what they ate,everything is wonderfully chronicled. The story involves real people as well,Benedict Arnold and Aaron Burr feature prominently. So it's researched day-to day life in them thar woods around 1775 and historical reporting of some early revolutionary war patriots. If you love to know what it was like to live in other times and if you're fantasies include a rough and rugged Daniel Boone type guy,then read this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11710251678952667808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70316703" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2710917-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70316703?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/2710917-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70316703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9650728780639133149"> Arundel is one of the finest books I have ever read. The characters come alive in such a manner that rereading the tale has been a yearly pleasure for me. The time period covered in "Arundel" is unique, our country before it was a country, only colonies and some men with ideas. The cast of characters is a mix of fictional and real, Benedict Arnold, Aaron Burr and others created in sync with the time and place. It is an epic tale of war, revenge, romance and friendship that entrances you from th</span>
  <span id="freeText9650728780639133149" style="display:none"> Arundel is one of the finest books I have ever read. The characters come alive in such a manner that rereading the tale has been a yearly pleasure for me. The time period covered in "Arundel" is unique, our country before it was a country, only colonies and some men with ideas. The cast of characters is a mix of fictional and real, Benedict Arnold, Aaron Burr and others created in sync with the time and place. It is an epic tale of war, revenge, romance and friendship that entrances you from the beginning. I don't recall every learning of Arnold's fateful March to Quebec in school, only that he was a traitor to The Cause. In "Arundel" you get a glimpse of just maybe who he really was. The tale also encompasses our own illusions of what we want, or think we want to be content and how all that works out in the end. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9650728780639133149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_74863863" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74863863" />

        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/2850357-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">historical fiction fans, revolutionary war buffs, romantics, and adventurers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2850357-john?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2850357-john?shelf=childrens-books-i-read-to-my-kids">childrens-books-i-read-to-my-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2850357-john?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2850357-john?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74863863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7437356653029872140">This is probably the best work of historical fiction I have ever read. It is the story of Benedict Arnold's March to Quebec (when Arnold was still a good guy), as seen through the eyes of a teenage boy who comes along. It's like Last of the Mohicans meets Annakin Skywalker. It blows the Harry Potter books away, and I say that as one who absolutely loved the Potter series. <br/><br/>Arundel is full of adventure, great characters, unforgettable scenery, romance, humor, just about everything you could hope f</span>
  <span id="freeText7437356653029872140" style="display:none">This is probably the best work of historical fiction I have ever read. It is the story of Benedict Arnold's March to Quebec (when Arnold was still a good guy), as seen through the eyes of a teenage boy who comes along. It's like Last of the Mohicans meets Annakin Skywalker. It blows the Harry Potter books away, and I say that as one who absolutely loved the Potter series. <br /><br />Arundel is full of adventure, great characters, unforgettable scenery, romance, humor, just about everything you could hope for in a book. And it is based on real history. Roberts used the journals of Arnold's soldiers as his primary source material. <br /><br />I started reading this last night to my 7 year old when he couldn't fall asleep. Looking forward enjoying this adventure again. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7437356653029872140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2973406126" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2973406126" />
    <a title="The Colonial" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/96806543-the-colonial"><img alt="The Colonial" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557196302p2/96806543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2973406126?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Colonial" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Colonial" href="/user/show/96806543-the-colonial">The Colonial</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2973406126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13107084910872411160">To write historical fiction and have it appeal to a wide audience in both its factual accuracy and story development is quite an undertaking, but to accomplish this feat and still have a relevant page-turning masterpiece almost one hundred years later is incomprehensible. Acclaimed novelist Kenneth Roberts shows his knack and skill for producing an epic that can proudly stand the test of time, using language and descriptions that are easily comprehended by generations of readers looking for nove</span>
  <span id="freeText13107084910872411160" style="display:none">To write historical fiction and have it appeal to a wide audience in both its factual accuracy and story development is quite an undertaking, but to accomplish this feat and still have a relevant page-turning masterpiece almost one hundred years later is incomprehensible. Acclaimed novelist Kenneth Roberts shows his knack and skill for producing an epic that can proudly stand the test of time, using language and descriptions that are easily comprehended by generations of readers looking for novella on the Revolutionary War.<br /><br />The story gets its title from the town of Arundel in the province of Maine, and chronicles the life and service of Steven Nason. Spanning his childhood spent in maritime and fishery trade on the coast as well as communicating and being on friendly terms with the local Native American Abenaki tribe, the book begins by taking us through the final years of the French and Indian War with Wolfe and Montcalm struggling for control of Quebec. It’s here that Roberts segways from history to the main plot in Steven’s knight-like pursuit of his love, a local Arundel girl kidnapped and rumored to be on her way to captivity in France. The fictional characters depicted are somewhat lacking in their depth, though it’s their experiences and insight on the steps leading up to and igniting the American Revolution that make their development that much more gripping and unique:<br /><br /><i>It seemed as though every man had a bitter grievance for which he longed to bash someone over the head; and through all the talk there ran the moan that our liberty was being taken from us, and that no nation or people had the right to steal liberty from other people. I could not help but see that those who had lost the least, or had the least to lose, being the poorest and wretchedest of our people, with little land, less money and no vote. Yet I learned from travelers that this was the way of it throughout New England.</i><br /><br />Roberts masterfully brings such rabble rousers as the Sons of Liberty, Benedict Arnold, and Daniel Morgan to the forefront, with mentions and appearances of Aaron Burr, Richard Montgomery, Ethan Allen, George Washington, and Robert Rogers to name but a few more. Arundel excels in its use of primary source material and research following both the Battle of the Plains of Abraham and the Battle of Quebec of 1775, with detailed narrative from Nason’s point of view illustrating the struggles, starvation, mishaps, and freezing temperatures that debilitated the Continentals’ invasion of Canada. Modern audiences may find the dialogue at times is aged and clumsy, with romance that tries too hard in its dramatic and emotional tone. Roberts is fair and exact on his measurements of Benedict Arnold’s bravery and tactical knowledge, and brings the history of the Battle of Quebec to the reader in an exciting and memorable fashion, leaving a desire for the sequel and his other books set in the Revolutionary period.<br /><br /><i>
  <a href="https://colonialreview.com/?p=919" rel="nofollow noopener">Read the Full Review and More</a>
</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13107084910872411160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2949245334" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2949245334" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48011083-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542289434p2/48011083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2949245334?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/48011083-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12814155325008654922">Some twenty-five years ago, while making small-talk at a client meeting, a senior executive asked if I had read Kenneth Robert’s <i>Arundel</i>. I was unfamiliar with both the book and the author. I filed that inquiry away into those usually untouched recesses within my brain. I say usually untouched, yet, that does not mean always untouched, for here we are, all these years later, to find this reader turning the pages of what book? Kenneth Robert’s <i>Arundel</i>.<br/><br/>This work is notable because of its historica</span>
  <span id="freeText12814155325008654922" style="display:none">Some twenty-five years ago, while making small-talk at a client meeting, a senior executive asked if I had read Kenneth Robert’s <i>Arundel</i>. I was unfamiliar with both the book and the author. I filed that inquiry away into those usually untouched recesses within my brain. I say usually untouched, yet, that does not mean always untouched, for here we are, all these years later, to find this reader turning the pages of what book? Kenneth Robert’s <i>Arundel</i>.<br /><br />This work is notable because of its historical perspective, Benedict Arnold’s campaign to Quebec. The characters, though, are unfortunately monochromatic. I thought much about the journals of Lewis and Clark; Roberts picks up on some of the experiences noted in the travels of the Corps of Discovery, most notably in interactions with various Indian tribes. Now reading Simone de Beauvoir’s <i>Second Sex</i>, I also caught a slight whiff of the Eternal Feminine here, though crudely rendered.<br /><br />As a literary work, this probably deserves, at best, 3 stars; given the historical perspective, however, I’ll bump it to 3 ½ stars. And since I’m feeling quite charitable, this being a holiday weekend, I’ll round my review up to 4 stars.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_767254333" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/767254333" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5070927-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307062026p2/5070927.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/767254333?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/5070927-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18114727-arundel">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of military adventure, wilderness tales, and meticulously researched historical fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=literary-peeps-2-fiction">literary-peeps-2-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=literary-peeps-3-epic">literary-peeps-3-epic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer767254333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2029428038110789046">This is how novels are supposed to be.<br/><br/>I came across this book by way of the Goodreads recommendation engine, which presented it to me when I was searching for "epics." The American Revolutionary War is far out of my time zone with respect to my own epic in progress, but since true epics are about the birth of nations and societies, and this book seemed to be very favorably viewed by critics, I thought I would give it a chance.<br/><br/>Am I ever glad I did. As soon as I downloaded the e-book to my Kindle </span>
  <span id="freeText2029428038110789046" style="display:none">This is how novels are supposed to be.<br /><br />I came across this book by way of the Goodreads recommendation engine, which presented it to me when I was searching for "epics." The American Revolutionary War is far out of my time zone with respect to my own epic in progress, but since true epics are about the birth of nations and societies, and this book seemed to be very favorably viewed by critics, I thought I would give it a chance.<br /><br />Am I ever glad I did. As soon as I downloaded the e-book to my Kindle and started reading the prologue, I was drawn in. The narrator and central character is one Steven Nason of Arundel (modern Kennebunkport) in the province of Maine, who, against his natural inclinations, is writing a book describing his role in Benedict Arnold's 1775 expedition to Quebec. He is driven reluctantly to this task by the grossly inaccurate tales that are current about the expedition and its members; he feels driven to set the record straight. In his own words:<blockquote>Above all, because of the lamentable occurrences at West Point, the countryside is filled with men of mighty hindsight who speak with scorn of Colonel Arnold, whose boots they were not fit to clean, and belittle or ignore the expedition to Quebec.</blockquote><br />What follows is a stirring tale of adventure, courage, passion, and humor, filled with surprises of all kinds.<br /><br />For reasons that in time are revealed, Nason starts the story in his own boyhood, describing the circumstances that brought his father, a blacksmith, to settle on the bank of the Arundel River, and describing his family's way of life there. Everything about it is striking and has the stamp of authenticity (the author, Kenneth Roberts, was something like a fourth-generation native of Maine): the trees, the weather, the ocean, the food, down to delightful surprises like young Steven's pet seal Eunice. These are frontier people, and in fact their property is surrounded by a palisade and he refers to their residence as a "garrison house." Steven's family are in close, intimate contact with the Abenakis, the local Indians.<br /><br />Indian characters and lifeways form a big part of the story. Roberts swings a wrecking-ball through the cliches of Indian-European relations and shows frontier society as a place where these two cultural groups collided, intermingled, interacted, and interbred. The Indian characters, like the white characters, are all distinct, vividly portrayed individuals with their own foibles and strengths. There is no monolithic them-versus-us consciousness, but rather a sense of individuals and groups acting from diverse motives as events swirl around them. When some Indians join the expedition to Quebec, their motives vary from personal loyalty to monetary gain to political calculation, among other things. In a book filled with memorable characters, one of the most vivid for me was the Indian Natanis, a superb woodsman who forms a strong bond with Steven and becomes an unsung hero of the expedition.<br /><br />Another memorable character is Benedict Arnold, stocky, athletic, temperamental, and passionate. Nowadays his name is proverbial for treachery, and indeed I knew nothing else about him but that, but in this story we see a man of shrewdness and courage, a natural leader whom Steven regards as second only to George Washington as a model officer.<br /><br />In brief, the story documents how Steven is engaged as a guide to the expedition, and how he travels with 1,000-odd ragtag American troops through the wilderness of Maine to reach Quebec City, which has been captured by the British. Among those who go with him are his burly, thieving friend Cap Huff, and a diminutive but resourceful and sharp-tongued local girl named Phoebe. Steven's own motivation is mostly personal: he knows that a girl he met in childhood, Mary Mallinson, is being held in Quebec and he intends to rescue her.<br /><br />Everyone knew that the expedition would be no cakewalk, but no one expected the indescribable ordeal that they actually face. Much of the novel is an account of that ordeal, and it is an amazing survival story, mixed in with a lot of infighting, intrigue, and suspicion.<br /><br />The quality of the writing itself is very high. Roberts is an intelligent, confident stylist who did a huge amount of research to get every detail right--and there are a lot of details. So everything has that wonderful, fresh, lifelike quality: unexpected but true. Steven Nason is a perfect narrator; he has great knowledge of what he's talking about, and a strong point of view that he's not shy about expressing. His plainspoken woodsman's perspective is often in contrast to the preening and dandyism of the more citified people he comes in contact with. Churlish and tongue-tied around women, he's incapable of expressing what he really feels. He needs their help.<br /><br />In sum, this is one of the best novels I have ever read. If I had to hunt for flaws in it, I might say that there was perhaps a bit much wilderness adventure for my taste. But it is a story of an expedition, after all--and it does pay off. This novel was published in 1930 and is about events in the 18th century, but it reads as though it were published yesterday and relates events that happened the day before that. The characters are vivid, present, and timeless. This must be what <i>classic</i> means, if it means anything.<br /><br />I serve notice to all my own readers, present and future: Kenneth Roberts is my role model. He is the standard I agree to be judged against. I won't measure up, but I will try.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2029428038110789046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2221263854" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2221263854" />
    <a title="Chris Gager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager"><img alt="Chris Gager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307204529p2/5295008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2221263854?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gager" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager">Chris Gager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/11456350-arundel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2221263854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6414373944550687711">It's been a LONG time since I read any Kenneth Roberts, a writer very popular among my parents generation. Early American Colonial historical fiction is his specialty. I've read "The Lively Lady" and "Boon Island" previously. This one starts out in Maine, down along the southern coast not far up from Kittery, where my parents lived for many years. I was looking for an interesting and adventurous read and it looks like I made the right choice. The cover image is correct for the book I have, but t</span>
  <span id="freeText6414373944550687711" style="display:none">It's been a LONG time since I read any Kenneth Roberts, a writer very popular among my parents generation. Early American Colonial historical fiction is his specialty. I've read "The Lively Lady" and "Boon Island" previously. This one starts out in Maine, down along the southern coast not far up from Kittery, where my parents lived for many years. I was looking for an interesting and adventurous read and it looks like I made the right choice. The cover image is correct for the book I have, but the background is much more blue-ish than the gray-blue pictured. BTW, there is still a town and Village of Arundel, Maine. It has not been replaced by Kennebunkport, but rather sits right to the north and east of Bush-land. It was not clear in my Maine Atlas where exactly the town line sits as you get closer to the ocean, but it looks like Kennebunkport was carved out of old Arundel, taking it's coastline. Or vice versa - see Wiki for clarification if you so desire it!<br /><br />Moving along as the baddies(French and Indian) have struck a blow. I assume this the fictional side of things, although random acts on nastiness did occur regularly along the lengthy interface between the French and British colonial territories. The narrator does point out that the French and Indian War was still dragging on and that nasty northern Indians and their French instigators were still a problem, even on the southern Maine coast. The massacre at Fort William Henry is mentioned. That's an important event in "The Last of the Mohicans" too. The specific French agent in this story points out cynically that when it come time for the colonists to fight for their freedom from England(coming VERY soon) their only allies will then be their current foes, the French. As we well know, he was right about that!<br /><br />Moving on with plenty going on, but the big story hasn't started yet. I do know that Steven does not marry Mary, as the promotional "extras" at the end of the book identify his wife as Phoebe. At this point she's a mere annoyance to him! One of the things I'm liking so far is the setting(s). Very close by to my own domicile and many familiar place names come into play. The Kennebec, the Androscoggin, Brunswick, Swan Island. Lots of good history to be learned. The author/narrator is not terribly sympathetic to white/European culture/settlers. Much more liking is sent the way of the Abenakis, who are pretty much gone nowadays. Sad, indeed ...<br /><br />Arnold's ill-fated Quebec trek has begun and Roberts spares now details on how challenging and miserable it was. In particular the weather was an obstacle. Not a bad time of year(Sept.-Oct.) ... depending. I remember climbing to the top(almost) of Saddleback(close to Arnold's route) one early November and it was a white-out blizzard. I went back a couple of weeks later and all the snow had been rained out and it was about 45 degrees and calm. Youneverknow ... Readers nowadays may grow impatient with the story as the author takes his time to paint a pretty detailed picture. Interesting to read of the differing attitudes of the white people toward the natives. Both Washington and Arnold seriously disliked and distrusted them - especially after the French and Indian war. I've stood on top of what Roberts calls Dead River Mountain(now called Bigelow) and looked down on Flagstaff Lake, whose creation covered over all those twists and turns of the Dead River that Steven complains about.<br /><br />- more local shoutouts - Georgetown, Arrowsic, Popham Beach<br /><br />- Major Return Meigs! No doubt an ancestor/relative of my neighbor Montgomery(Monty) Meigs Smith. He also had a famous Civil War General Meigs ancestor. The guy who created Arlington cemetery.<br /><br />Will finish this tale tonight. The Arnold "Brigade" has finally made it to the Plains of Abraham with 2/3 of its original strength. Many left behind(and many of those dead) who succumbed to malnutrition and exhaustion, not to mention disease. What a mess. Roberts strongly implies that the expedition was dogged by spies and sabotage. I guess historical research must have revealed this. The lads will not be able to "take" Quebec(City) despite getting help. They will go on to glory in Upper New York at the critical battle of Saratoga. I assume that's not a part of this book, except for being mentioned a few times. BTW, a doggie gets killed and eaten by some hard-up troops. You eat a dog ... your going to hell. IMHO!<br /><br />Finished with this sturdy, exciting tale last night. The historical accuracy is strong, as I assume it is in all of KR's books. The sequel to this book is "Rabble in Arms," which follows the further adventures of Cap, Steven, Phoebe et al during the northeast portion of the Revolutionary War in America. The only sort of "issue" with the books is the out and out fictional stuff: the Phoebe-Steven-Mary romantic triangle, the broad and boring antics of Cap(think Victor McLaglen in John Ford's western movies) and the speculation through prose about the personalities of Washinton, Burr, and Arnold. Other historical characters(Daniel Morgan) are more shallowly and straight-forwardly presented. An excellent book if you can stand the slog. How ironic that Steven's "gang" has such an easy time for it in their winter-time return to Arundel over the same path that Arnold's army took. All those obstacles were frozen and covered in snow.<br /><br />- 4.25* rounds down to 4* - Mr. Roberts is solid, but he's not Hilary Mantel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6414373944550687711" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

What Arnold's forces endured during their journey through Maine is a story of steely determination and bravery in the face of unimaginable hardships and it's one that should not be forgotten. </span>

<div id="review_3251286000" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Dannica Zulestin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28789176-dannica-zulestin"><img alt="Dannica Zulestin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428105952p2/28789176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3251286000?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannica Zulestin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannica Zulestin" href="/user/show/28789176-dannica-zulestin">Dannica Zulestin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28789176-dannica-zulestin?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3251286000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1225045986351996488">This was given to me a couple days ago as a very belated Christmas present. I think the giver should have held out for my birthday, which is only in two weeks. But it was a good book to read during slow periods at work, and a pretty good book in general.<br/><br/>Some thoughts:<br/>-A book that makes me want to know just what the fuck happened with Benedict Arnold anyways, given that he's a decent colonel in this book and I don't actually know the story of that whole betrayal thing.<br/><br/>-For a book published in 193</span>
  <span id="freeText1225045986351996488" style="display:none">This was given to me a couple days ago as a very belated Christmas present. I think the giver should have held out for my birthday, which is only in two weeks. But it was a good book to read during slow periods at work, and a pretty good book in general.<br /><br />Some thoughts:<br />-A book that makes me want to know just what the fuck happened with Benedict Arnold anyways, given that he's a decent colonel in this book and I don't actually know the story of that whole betrayal thing.<br /><br />-For a book published in 1930, it's kind of progressive? I'm not super confident analyzing racial dynamics in historical fiction, but Roberts writes interesting Abenaki characters--I couldn't say, from Natanis and Hobomok and Jacataqua, who would be my favorite, but they're all different and really fun. It's not a book where all Indians are the same, or all of them are hostile, or where the noble savage trope is used--well, for the most part, though the narrator does occasionally make statements such as saying that Indians are generally more honest than white people, but historically speaking that's kind of true. Some characters in Arundel are racist, it's true, but this is clearly shown to be a flaw--in some cases laughable (Cap, for example) and in other cases devastating (Washington and Arnold being unwilling to trust Abenaki guides going up the Kennebec, to awful effect). And aside from racial dynamics, there's Phoebe, who's the main female character and a woman who co-owns and captains a sloop, and is tough enough to travel up to Quebec with all the rest of the army.<br /><br />-Despite saying Phoebe's characterization and actions are kind of progressive, I still didn't like her romance with Steven that much! In the beginning of the book, she's outright rude, and her relationship with Steven throughout the book is the sort where antagonism grows into love--but the antagonism lasts for so long that I have a hard time buying the love in the end.<br /><br />-At the beginning I was really bored by Steven going on about Maine food and Maine weather and such, but when I started getting hungry on shift, I felt very invested in all of Steven's descriptions of food and the times when it was hard to get or army rations were running low or wrecked by a storm. RELATABLE. I too want food constantly.<br /><br />Anyways it was a pretty good book but also the prose is pretty thick for an adventure novel so I might not read the sequel right away even though we do have it in the house.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1225045986351996488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Kenneth Roberts can make you SEE the beautiful circumstances of Arundel and make you FEEL the joys and hatred and heartbreak and love of Steven Nason.   <span id="freeText12713939634591016803" style="display:none">Tagor once said that books are nothing but black and white prisons which imprisons the past...<br />This book is the perfect example of the quote quoted above. Kenneth Roberts can make you SEE the beautiful circumstances of Arundel and make you FEEL the joys and hatred and heartbreak and love of Steven Nason. Now, I am no fan of battles and wars but I do understand the necessity of independence and how man will do anything to gain it. The adventurous journey made by Nason and the army under Arnold was just a perfect portrait of such expeditions... to gain it and though not successful, they didn't lose hope ...<br />Roberts always portrays true human nature and he did it here too. By the way, this book totally deserved the Pulitzer it won.</span>

<div id="review_2013417146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2013417146" />
    <a title="Stephen Brooke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6900922-stephen-brooke"><img alt="Stephen Brooke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525033245p2/6900922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2013417146?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Brooke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Brooke" href="/user/show/6900922-stephen-brooke">Stephen Brooke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6900922-stephen-brooke?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2013417146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2853043388726054854">First, the grumbles: Roberts seems to be fond of his own voice and sometimes goes around and around before getting to things. The characters he introduces are often closely described but there seems little to them beyond that, no depth, just surface. Motivations are questionable. The romantic scenarios are a bit laughable and the whole kidnapping-of-Mary that unifies the plot doesn’t make much sense.<br/><br/>When it comes to portraying a time and a place, however, the novel excels. “Arundel” is top-notch</span>
  <span id="freeText2853043388726054854" style="display:none">First, the grumbles: Roberts seems to be fond of his own voice and sometimes goes around and around before getting to things. The characters he introduces are often closely described but there seems little to them beyond that, no depth, just surface. Motivations are questionable. The romantic scenarios are a bit laughable and the whole kidnapping-of-Mary that unifies the plot doesn’t make much sense.<br /><br />When it comes to portraying a time and a place, however, the novel excels. “Arundel” is top-notch historical fiction — the causes of the Revolution are better explained than in many a history book, and the glimpses of Native American life are well done. It is not quite one story but a series of connected ones, episodes in the narrator’s life in colonial New England (primarily Maine), through both the French and Indian and the Revolutionary Wars.<br /><br />An interesting enough read most of the time, despite the occasional tedious stretches that could be well have been condensed with no loss to the story (but important, I suppose, to thorough historical accuracy). Recommended more to those interested in the history of the period and willing to deal with a not-particularly-focused plot, than to the reader seeking a fast-paced adventure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2853043388726054854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_810938717" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/810938717" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3677799-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293326478p2/3677799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/810938717?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/3677799-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/1141071.Arundel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3677799-mary?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3677799-mary?shelf=us-history">us-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer810938717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14004012200386425033">I picked this book off my father's bookshelf. I am not sure how I missed it 50 years ago, or if it just recently appeared in his living room. When I asked Dad about it, he said that he read it in the 1930s as a boy, that it was very popular, and that he liked it a lot. I liked it a lot too. It is one of the most enjoyable books I have ever read. It was such a pleasure to read about ordinary people who are depicted with crystalline clarity in all their heroic strengths and foolish weaknesses. The</span>
  <span id="freeText14004012200386425033" style="display:none">I picked this book off my father's bookshelf. I am not sure how I missed it 50 years ago, or if it just recently appeared in his living room. When I asked Dad about it, he said that he read it in the 1930s as a boy, that it was very popular, and that he liked it a lot. I liked it a lot too. It is one of the most enjoyable books I have ever read. It was such a pleasure to read about ordinary people who are depicted with crystalline clarity in all their heroic strengths and foolish weaknesses. The bad guys are motivated by the usual normal vices. (Oh, for the good ol' days of the seven deadly sins driving antagonistic human behavior without any hint of brain-chemistry induced psycho/socio pathology a la a recently read novel by Dean Koontz.) <br />The story revolves around Benedict Arnold's torturous expedition with a rabble of an ill-equipped army through the Maine wilderness to assault and take Quebec in 1775. Through the voice of Stephen Nason, a young settler in coastal Maine, Kenneth Roberts depicts his characters with compassion, respect, love, and humor. Stephen's quest for the infatuatee of his life energizes his scouting efforts for the army. Betrayal is woven very democratically through relationships and events, and everyone suffers from the extreme Maine winter weather which Roberts makes the reader experience through Stephen's narration. There is a refreshing unselfconscious lack of stereotyping whether speaking of women, indigenous people, French Canadians, farmers and merchants turned soldiers, and even Benedict Arnold. <br />Roberts researched details using many local (Maine) sources which are referenced at the back of the book. I was also happy to discover some recommended books there and that Roberts has a few more books drawing on the same characters. Time to get online with my library to see what is actually available.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14004012200386425033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1423868-bruce?shelf=historical">historical</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51438928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10200054592373414710">A couple years ago while we were up visiting our friends in Maine, we ventured into a used bookstore. I don't recall what I bought, but my friend found a copy of a book and purchased it. As we left the shop, he handed the book to me and said that he thought that I would really enjoy it. I do recall his saying it was a work of fiction although based on historical fact. He and I are both fans of George MacDonald Fraser's Flashman books, which are in the same genre.<br/>It wasn't much later that I got m</span>
  <span id="freeText10200054592373414710" style="display:none">A couple years ago while we were up visiting our friends in Maine, we ventured into a used bookstore. I don't recall what I bought, but my friend found a copy of a book and purchased it. As we left the shop, he handed the book to me and said that he thought that I would really enjoy it. I do recall his saying it was a work of fiction although based on historical fact. He and I are both fans of George MacDonald Fraser's Flashman books, which are in the same genre.<br />It wasn't much later that I got my first Kindle and got hooked on reading digital books. My physical book collection has been collecting dust, but before heading to the beach, I decided it was probably wiser to take a physical book, than the Kindle Reader. Arundel was the first book that came to mind, and boy, am I glad it was!<br />By the end of the chapter I was hooked! The characters are just so well written that you almost feel you know them. One of the characters, Cap Huff, is a bit of a lovable scoundrel, and as such, easily one of my favorite characters. <br />But anyway, the story of Benedict Arnold's expedition to surprise the English at Quebec is an amazing story and damned hard to put down! Add to that the story of Steven Nason's quest to rescue his lost love, and you've got a winner that I will easily give 5 stars on Goodreads. Just on that alone, I can't imagine this book hasn't been scooped up to make a teen angst movie (and no, it doesn't read that way, I can just see how easily it could be played that way).<br />Highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10200054592373414710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136346591?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/1370632-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136346591" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1655748104942069843">My wife read this first and told me I'd like it. And I did. I don't read a ton of historical fiction, but I was a history major and mainly focused on 17th-19th century America (well, as much as an undergrad major can be focused anyway), so this seemed like it would be a fun read. It is.<br/><br/>It's narrated by Steven Nason, who is from Arundel, Maine (now Kennebunkport), who leaves his home to become a guide/soldier in the Continental Army under Benedict Arnold as Arnold is charged by George Washington </span>
  <span id="freeText1655748104942069843" style="display:none">My wife read this first and told me I'd like it. And I did. I don't read a ton of historical fiction, but I was a history major and mainly focused on 17th-19th century America (well, as much as an undergrad major can be focused anyway), so this seemed like it would be a fun read. It is.<br /><br />It's narrated by Steven Nason, who is from Arundel, Maine (now Kennebunkport), who leaves his home to become a guide/soldier in the Continental Army under Benedict Arnold as Arnold is charged by George Washington to take Quebec from the British.<br /><br />It's not so much written as a page-turner but the book just moves along, just pulling you with it so you discover you've read 100 or so pages without even knowing the time has passed. And then you want to read more. There are all sorts of examples of dry, droll New England wit, with plenty of puns here and there. One of my favorites is when the young Steven and his father meet Arnold, before the war, and swap animal pelts for a short passage on Arnold's boat. After the deal is made, his father says to Steven, of Arnold: "That boy's a trader, Stevie. That was good trading you listened to, and your pa sort of out-traded him."<br /><br />Finally, this book was enjoyable enough that I was happy to see that there was a sequel with the further adventures of these characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1655748104942069843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4962047064181386222">This 1929 historical fiction novel about the American Revolution has not been made into a movie yet and I do not know why. It's a dense, academic read, but if you can read slowly and carefully, you'll find such rich characters and very funny lines -- especially those observing women. It might be my new favorite book. And it also highlights why Americans always go for the underdog -- we WERE the underdog. The other thing I like is that it's a quest book but on familiar landscape -- the trees and </span>
  <span id="freeText4962047064181386222" style="display:none">This 1929 historical fiction novel about the American Revolution has not been made into a movie yet and I do not know why. It's a dense, academic read, but if you can read slowly and carefully, you'll find such rich characters and very funny lines -- especially those observing women. It might be my new favorite book. And it also highlights why Americans always go for the underdog -- we WERE the underdog. The other thing I like is that it's a quest book but on familiar landscape -- the trees and animals and such are all in MY backyard. Lots of quest books have hobbits and things I don't know well. This one is true. Loved it.</span>

<div id="review_3519535369" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3519535369" />
    <a title="Dana Clinton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32568806-dana-clinton"><img alt="Dana Clinton" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3519535369?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dana Clinton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dana Clinton" href="/user/show/32568806-dana-clinton">Dana Clinton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3519535369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8515669925215841531">Kenneth Roberts was awarded a special Pulitzer Prize citation for having "long contributed to the creation of greater interest in our early American History". His first book, published in 1930, was Arundel, an almost 500 pages of dense reading selected for the September read of my local library book group, which met on Zoom last night. The action takes place in 1775, so before the colonies broke away from England. Maine was (is) a vast territory mostly of wilderness, so it was of particular inte</span>
  <span id="freeText8515669925215841531" style="display:none">Kenneth Roberts was awarded a special Pulitzer Prize citation for having "long contributed to the creation of greater interest in our early American History". His first book, published in 1930, was Arundel, an almost 500 pages of dense reading selected for the September read of my local library book group, which met on Zoom last night. The action takes place in 1775, so before the colonies broke away from England. Maine was (is) a vast territory mostly of wilderness, so it was of particular interest to me, now living in southern Maine, to discover more of the history of this period. That was the reward; the reading seemed to take forever, even to me who doesn't balk at long books! I was under pressure to finish it before the discussion, which I did... but it left me feeling strained! The narrator, Steven Nason, recounts his early years in Arundel (which today is mostly Portland), his family which ran the local garrison inn, and his encounters with various people who play a part in his tale. He "falls in love" with Mary when they are both quite young, and when she is captured and hauled off to Quebec after seeing her father murdered, he is determined to bring her back. So this gives the tale cohesion but ends up being such a minor element of the tale. Years later as a young man, Steve joins up with Benedict Arnold, directed by George Washington to lead a surprise attack on Quebec City, controlled at that time by the British. The surprise would be approaching the city through the vast wilderness of Maine, considered impossible for an army. Over 300 pages detail in almost painful (but fascinating) detail the belabored and disaster-ridden march up the Kennebec and other rivers, the bogs and rapids and starvation ever present. Roberts gives the reader so much information not only about the politics of the time, but of the relations between white settlers and the Abenaki nation (some hostile, some friendly, and several native Americans are central to the success of the raid as well as to the survival of most of our main characters). He clearly admired the native Americans and hated the atrocities perpetrated upon them. "It may be I shall be damned for saying so; but unless I have misread my Bible, I have found more Christianity and human kindness in Rabomis and Hobomok, the m'téoulin, and Natawammet and Mogg Chabonoke and Natanis and other of my Indian friends than in the venerated and violent Cotton Mather of Boston, who had declared in his writings that all red men are Scythians, and that the practicing of cruelty on them and the breaking of treaties with them are justified in God's sight." The detailed presentation of Quebec City and its surroundings is also so well-researched. Some in the book group felt this was a history book, not a fiction book, but no, it was the best of historical fiction... incredible research and descriptions, but many main characters invented in spite of so many of the characters being drawn from truth. It was well worth the read, but I am not ready to tackle any more of the adventures in this series!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8515669925215841531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3107154999" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3107154999" />
    <a title="Steven Hull" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26497298-steven-hull"><img alt="Steven Hull" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3107154999?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Hull" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Hull" href="/user/show/26497298-steven-hull">Steven Hull</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3107154999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11583917509558950020"> Arundel is Kenneth Roberts’s first commercially successful novel. With it, he set a pattern of how he approached historical fiction. His subsequent books reflected this pattern. First and foremost, Roberts set a high bar for historical research as a cornerstone of his effort. Although not trained as a historian, his adherence to historical accuracy adds credence to his content and enhances the fictional attractiveness of Arundel. <br/>	Roberts confines this story primarily to the American invasion o</span>
  <span id="freeText11583917509558950020" style="display:none"> Arundel is Kenneth Roberts’s first commercially successful novel. With it, he set a pattern of how he approached historical fiction. His subsequent books reflected this pattern. First and foremost, Roberts set a high bar for historical research as a cornerstone of his effort. Although not trained as a historian, his adherence to historical accuracy adds credence to his content and enhances the fictional attractiveness of Arundel. <br />	Roberts confines this story primarily to the American invasion of Canada and attack on Quebec in the fall and winter of 1775. This is a book of larger than life characters like Benedict Arnold and Cap Huff, of extraordinary women, of enduring love, and disappointment, and of super human effort. <br />	The center of attention and the narrator is Steven Nason. Steven and his progeny will appear in subsequent Roberts novels. There is Natanis and other native Americans, who display unrivaled wilderness skills and loyalty. It is the heroic march to Quebec that is the center stage of the book, a march that actually occurred. The hero of the march and of the book is Benedict Arnold. This is the first of Roberts’s attempts to give credibility to a name in American history synonymous with treason. The Arnold we know in Arundel is a superb athlete, an effective leader, one who overcomes every challenge, and a brave patriot. <br />	In the end winter, the difficulty of the march, and the loss of so many men prior to the attempt to take the city undo Arnold’s and his men’s efforts. Nason’s multi-year love affair with Mary ends but his loving relationship with the hardy, smart, and tough Phoebe is firmly established. The book ends with many possibilities for a sequel, which ultimately Roberts will provide. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11583917509558950020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2121926890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6097183408916377806">Fantastically written fictionalized tale of the failed attempt to take Quebec by Benedict Arnold and Daniel Morgan in the winter of 1775, told by a young man from Arundel(now Kennebunkport) Maine. The reader feels not only the bone freezing cold of the river crossings, but also the lack of food, warm clothing, and decent maps. There is a new appreciation of all that the Native Americans did to help the Patriot Army. Amazing descriptions of Revolutionary War main players, especially Benedict Arno</span>
  <span id="freeText6097183408916377806" style="display:none">Fantastically written fictionalized tale of the failed attempt to take Quebec by Benedict Arnold and Daniel Morgan in the winter of 1775, told by a young man from Arundel(now Kennebunkport) Maine. The reader feels not only the bone freezing cold of the river crossings, but also the lack of food, warm clothing, and decent maps. There is a new appreciation of all that the Native Americans did to help the Patriot Army. Amazing descriptions of Revolutionary War main players, especially Benedict Arnold and Aaron Burr. It is clear that Arnold was a brave and charismatic leader. Roberts' tale is based on his own ancestor's involvement in this heroic, although failed attempt to pluck Quebec from British rule. My brother gave me this book for my birthday. It is one of his favorite books and it is now one of mine. </span>

<div id="review_2604213376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2604213376" />
    <a title="Pete Iseppi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12710436-pete-iseppi"><img alt="Pete Iseppi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347296475p2/12710436.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2604213376?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pete Iseppi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pete Iseppi" href="/user/show/12710436-pete-iseppi">Pete Iseppi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2604213376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2374178894385185993">This is an early historical novel, written in the 1930's. It's the first of a trilogy, and I enjoyed it. One of the interesting parts of this book, is how much the author admires Benedict Arnold. (This story takes place during the American Revolution.) I think that most of us think of Arnold only as a traitor to his country. This novel makes me want to ready a biography of Arnold.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9sEFm4IFhdTp5RmFgzDIiS7t4aJfRpF9daQFgI6IRDw3CTDSfkTSJaTPEBJe1IOMkF+VBcVBDJNvTUnQvcPUNQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PLxoWo2zCIAqs2J98ANZbmOUxwz6wVsUvXn0HPkQ5Dj9dF0TcfJfcWeZa+ot5xJr3Sazq2DGxvqnkLhMylt0MQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Thirty-three years later comes an illustrated, corrected, revised and greatly expanded new edition... Once there was a time--and it was not so long ago--when radio listening, especially to the dramas, was one of the most important events in many a young person\'s life. People developed a love affair with the radio, and though the old times are now gone forever, the love affair continues. The heroes and settings of radio drama spurred the imagination to supply its own and much better images than visual media provided. There were no padded shoulders on the Lone Ranger, Superman flew with no jiggly trick photography, and the Martians whom Orson Welles helped attack the Earth were more convincing than anything the movies can provide. For those who have been under the thrall of radio\'s alluring call, your new host Jim Harmon provides reminiscences of the heyday of radio programming, with insights on such radio dramas as I Love a Mystery, Gangbusters, The Shadow, Inner Sanctum, Batman and Robin, Superman, Tom Mix, The Lone Ranger, The Green Hornet, Adventures by Morse and a couple of dozen more. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3468574267291979795">Kenneth Lewis Roberts was an American author of historical novels. Roberts worked first as a journalist, becoming nationally known for his work with the <i>Saturday Evening Post</i> from 1919 to 1928, and then as a popular novelist. Born in Kennebunk, Maine, Roberts specialized in Regionalist historical fiction. He often wrote about his native state and its terrain, also depicting other upper New England</span>
  <span id="freeText3468574267291979795" style="display:none">Kenneth Lewis Roberts was an American author of historical novels. Roberts worked first as a journalist, becoming nationally known for his work with the <i>Saturday Evening Post</i> from 1919 to 1928, and then as a popular novelist. Born in Kennebunk, Maine, Roberts specialized in Regionalist historical fiction. He often wrote about his native state and its terrain, also depicting other upper New England states and scenes. For example, the main characters in <i>Arundel</i> and <i>Rabble in Arms</i> are from Kennebunk (then called Arundel), the main character of <i> Northwest Passage</i> is depicted as being from Kittery, Maine with friends in Portsmouth, New Hampshire, and the main character in <i>Oliver Wiswell</i> is from Milton, Massachusetts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3468574267291979795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
